    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="60031bbc82d84c1a9f52dca68af1bda5">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '452d3622-a9f3-4e3b-bad1-c08710a5ba34'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d151980028" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Titanates have been widely reported as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, their wide temperature suitability and cycle life remain fundamental issues that hinder their practical application. Herein, a novel hollow Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> microsphere (H-NTO) with a unique chemically bonded NTO/C(N) interface is reported. Theoretical calculations demonstrated that the NTO/C(N) interface stabilizes the crystal structure, and the optimized interface enables the H-NTO anode to stably operate for 80&nbsp;000 cycles in a conventional ester electrolyte with negligible capacity loss. Optimizing the electrolyte allows the H-NTO electrode to cycle stably for 200 calendar days without capacity degradation at −40&nbsp;°C. The excellent cycling stability is attributed to the NTO/C(N) interface and the stable solid electrolyte interphase formed by the highly adaptable electrolyte/electrode interface. Titanate exhibits solvent co-intercalation behavior in ether-based electrolytes, and its robust structure ensures that it can adapt to large volume changes at low temperatures. This study provides a unique perspective on the long-cycle mechanism of titanate anodes and highlights the critical importance of manipulating the interfacial chemistry in SIBs, including the material and electrode/electrolyte interfaces.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202301376-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202301376-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), a new energy storage technology, are widely considered promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), owing to the abundant source of Na ore and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0001_R_d151980020e371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The anode materials of commercial LIBs exhibit a poor Na-storage capacity (31&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) in ester-based electrolytes, and lower insertion voltages tend to lead to Na plating and dendrites, causing safety hazards.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0002_R_d151980020e378" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Titanium-based materials have attracted the interest researchers because of their high activity, low cost, and environmental friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0003_R_d151980020e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Energy Environ. Sci.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0004_R_d151980020e384" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering its specific capacity and minimum discharge voltage factor, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NTO) is considered an ideal anode material with the highest safety and energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0005_R_d151980020e396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NTO had a jagged lamellar framework of TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra connected by edges. The energy required for Na ions to pass through a layer is substantially higher than the energy required for them to travel along that layer. In addition, the wide bandgap (3.7&nbsp;eV) also results in the low electronic conductivity of NTO, resulting in a slow electron migration rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0006_R_d151980020e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0007_R_d151980020e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These factors lead to slow Na reaction kinetics and limit the rate performance of NTO as an anode material in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0005_R_d151980020e408" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Researchers have taken many steps to address these issues; however, several problems remain. For example, surface coatings cannot change the inherent conductivity of the material and may cause phase interface side reactions and hinder the Na ion extraction process, thus affecting the capacity of the material itself. Therefore, many aspects deserve further exploration and research, including attempts to combine two or more modification methods to improve the electrochemical performance of NTO, such as defects in conjunction with doping, or double doping or co-doping methods.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0008_R_d151980020e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a>-<a href="#adma202301376-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0010_R_d151980020e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In recent years, the controllable generation of oxygen vacancies in materials has enabled the sustainable development of high-performance materials, and the introduction and modulation of vacancies have received significant attention in many fields. They have become a research hotspot because of the effects of vacancies on the structure and physical/chemical properties of materials, particularly in the enhanced energy storage of metal oxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0011_R_d151980020e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a>-<a href="#adma202301376-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0013_R_d151980020e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Many methods for introducing vacancies in metal-oxide electrode materials exist, including high-temperature vapor-phase reduction, solvothermal methods, and etching, which have been successfully applied to chalcogenide oxides, TiO<sub>2,</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub>, and have significantly improved the electrode ion-diffusion coefficient and electrochemical reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0013_R_d151980020e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0014_R_d151980020e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Heteroelement doping strategies can fundamentally modulate the electronic structure and change the polarity of a material, providing new properties to the material and enhancing its electrochemical performance. Currently, various nonmetallic heteroelements, including N, O, S, B, and P, can be doped into carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0002_R_d151980020e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0010_R_d151980020e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">10</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0015_R_d151980020e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0016_R_d151980020e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It was found that the doping configuration and content significantly influenced the material properties. Properly designed morphologies and structures, such as 2D nanosheets<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0017_R_d151980020e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and hollow structures, <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0012_R_d151980020e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> can also effectively mitigate volume changes, maintain structural integrity, and promote ion transport, leading to a stable cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0018_R_d151980020e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Nano">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we report the design and preparation of hollow NTO microspheres (H-NTO) with oxygen defects, fabricated from 2D ultrathin nanosheets with a unique chemically bonded NTO/C(N) interface. The hollow structure provided a larger specific surface area, offered more electrode/electrolyte contact interfaces, and shortened the ion/electron transfer diffusion channels. The ultrathin nanosheets reduced the number of NTO layers and reduced aggregation and overlap owing to the surface energy, while also providing a better ion-diffusion path. The oxygen vacancies provide more active sites for Na ions, enhancing the electrical conductivity and reducing the diffusion potential of Na ions between the NTO layers. The unique chemically bonded interface improved electrical conductivity and stabilized the framework structure. As expected, H-NTO exhibited high specific capacity (120&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 3000 cycles) and excellent rate performance (59&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) when used as an anode material for SIBs, with particularly outstanding performance in terms of cycle life, which were record test results (330 days cycling at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 80000 cycles at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). By optimizing the electrolyte, we also achieved the long-cycle life of NTO electrodes in the all-weather range (25 000 cycles at 60&nbsp;°C and 3000 cycles at −40&nbsp;°C), demonstrating the feasibility of NTO as an anode material for SIBs in practical applications. This study emphasizes the importance of interface, defect, and electrolyte engineering in the fabrication of highly durable Ti-based anode materials and wide-operation-temperature batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202301376-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202301376-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202301376-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202301376-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Synthesis and Structural Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The preparation path for NTO is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S5</a> (Supporting Information)). The obtained NTO was annealed under different atmospheres to obtain the final materials (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0001">1a</a>, the samples obtained by annealing in Ar/H<sub>2</sub>, Ar, and air are named H-NTO, A-NTO, and C-NTO, respectively). The morphology and structure of the prepared materials were examined using field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0001">1b,c</a> show that the particle sizes of the synthesized samples were homogeneous. All samples have a spherical morphology with a diameter of ≈1&nbsp;µm and several 2D nanosheets covering the surface, indicating that the original morphology was well maintained during calcination. The TEM images in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0001">1d,e</a> reveal that H-NTO has hollow structures inside and ultrathin 2D nanosheets on the surface, with clusters of approximately eight layers. The A-NTO and C-NTO samples also exhibited morphologies similar to that of H-NTO (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6-S7</a>, Supporting Information). High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images clearly show a layer spacing of 0.285&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (−211) crystal plane of H-NTO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0001">1f</a>). Three different diffraction rings attributed to the (001), (101), and (301) lattice planes were observed based on the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) maps in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0001">1g</a>, further confirming the NTO phase. The high-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) mapping image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0001">1h</a> shows that Ti, C, N, O, and Na were uniformly distributed in the microspheres. Energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) and inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) showed that the Na:Ti ratio was ≈2:4.1 for the H-NTO samples (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202301376-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3d2da0bc-e47d-429c-9f50-c63b05db09a2/adma202301376-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3d2da0bc-e47d-429c-9f50-c63b05db09a2/adma202301376-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b0054a5b-1e76-4467-8502-9f006966c410/adma202301376-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3d2da0bc-e47d-429c-9f50-c63b05db09a2/adma202301376-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202301376-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202301376" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphology and structural characterization of H-NTO microspheres. a) Schematic diagram of synthetic route for H-NTO microspheres. b,c) FESEM images, d,e) TEM images, f) HRTEM image, g) SAED pattern, h) HAADF-STEM image and corresponding mapping patterns.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The crystal structure of the materials was characterized using X-ray diffraction (XRD). As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0002">2a</a>, no significant differences in the characteristic peaks of NTO annealed in different atmospheres were observed, indicating that the annealing atmosphere did not damage the structure or crystalline phases. All samples showed three characteristic diffraction peaks at 10.5°, 25.7°, and 47.8°, respectively, corresponding to the (001), (011), and (020) crystal planes of NTO (JCPDS. 31-1329) .<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0002_R_d151980020e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0005_R_d151980020e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The small convex packet at ≈26° can be attributed to the amorphous carbon derived during annealing. All Raman spectra showed the same shapes, and the different annealing atmospheres did not significantly affect the Raman peaks of the material (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The defective structure of the materials was characterized by electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0002">2b</a>, the H-NTO sample had the largest concentration of oxygen vacancies, which was caused by the detachment and absence of oxygen in the NTO lattice under the dual reduction effect of high temperature and hydrogen and the formation of oxygen vacancies (for a more detailed explanation, see the Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0012_R_d151980020e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0019_R_d151980020e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small">19</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0020_R_d151980020e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater. 2022">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When annealed in Ar or air, the number of oxygen vacancies on the material surface is much lower than that of H-NTO.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202301376-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b230b330-4186-4928-87d6-46fe559d3479/adma202301376-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b230b330-4186-4928-87d6-46fe559d3479/adma202301376-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4361a73a-4af3-4b95-a053-8425b2927cab/adma202301376-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b230b330-4186-4928-87d6-46fe559d3479/adma202301376-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202301376-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202301376" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of the NTO-base active materials. a) XRD pattern and b) EPR spectrum for C-NTO, A-NTO, and H-NTO samples. c–f) The high-resolution XPS spectra for Ti 2p (c), O 1s (d), C 1s (e), and N 1s (f) &nbsp;for H-NTO samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The electronic state surfaces and chemical compositions of all samples were studied by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The survey spectra confirmed the presence of Ti, O, Na, C, and N (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), which agreed with the HAADF-STEM mapping. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0002">2c</a>, typical Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p3/2 and 2p1/2 located at 458.3&nbsp;eV and 464.2&nbsp;eV, respectively, and two relatively weak peaks appearing at 457.7 and 463&nbsp;eV, corresponding to Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p3/2 and Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p1/2, respectively, indicate the appearance of oxygen vacancies on the surface of H-NTO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0021_R_d151980020e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Electrochim. Acta">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is confirmed by the O 1s XPS spectrum of O<sub>v</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0002">2d</a>) .<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0022_R_d151980020e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the C 1s XPS spectrum of H-NTO, the peaks at 289.5, 288.8, 285.5, and 284.1&nbsp;eV are attributed to O<span class="icomoon"></span>C≐O, C<span class="icomoon"></span>O, C<span class="icomoon"></span>C, and C≐C bonds, respectively. In addition, an additional peak at a binding energy of 282.3&nbsp;eV, significantly higher than the binding energy of Ti<sup>4+</sup>─C, can be attributed to Ti<sup>3+</sup>─C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0002">2e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0006_R_d151980020e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A N─Ti bond at 396&nbsp;eV was observed in the N 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0002">2f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0023_R_d151980020e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nanomicro Lett.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS results revealed a unique NTO/C(N) chemically bonded interface. Based on these features, a possible formation process for the partially bound NTO/C(N) hybrids is proposed. With the onset of the solvothermal process, titanium (IV) isopropoxide is hydrolyzed in an isopropanol solvent to form titanium dioxide covered with numerous ─OH hydroxides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0022_R_d151980020e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These functional groups can interact with the surface of O<sub>bulk</sub>–Ti–OH during the solvothermal treatment with isopropanol and undergo a series of condensation and recombination processes, resulting in a chemically bonded TiO<sub>2</sub>/C(N) interface (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). After the conversion of TiO<sub>2</sub> to NTO, a final chemically bonded NTO/C(N) interface was formed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0006_R_d151980020e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS profiles of the A-NTO samples exhibited similar results (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, similar behavior was not observed in the C-NTO sample (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Therefore, we speculate that the formation of C(N)<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti bonds may be related to the oxygen vacancies in the material. We increased the annealing temperature to 650&nbsp;°C and prepared C-NTO-650 and H-NTO-650 in air and an Ar/H<sub>2</sub> mixture, respectively, and the XPS tests of the obtained samples are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14,<span class="smallCaps">S15</span></a> <span class="smallCaps">(</span>Supporting Information<span class="smallCaps">)</span>. C(N)<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti bonds can still be observed in H-NTO-650, while C-NTO-650 is entirely free of C(N)<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti bonds, indicating that C (N) atoms can occupy the oxygen vacancies generated to form the NTO/C (N) interface, which has also been reported in the related literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0024_R_d151980020e677" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>UV-vis absorption spectra and XPS valence band spectroscopy further confirmed the enhanced electrical conductivity. The band gap energies (<i>E</i><sub>g</sub>) of all materials were also investigated by UV-vis absorption spectroscopy, and it was observed that the absorption edges of A-NTO and H-NTO had shifted to 374 and 387&nbsp;nm, respectively, when compared to C-NTO (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting Information); therefore, the <i>E</i><sub>g</sub> of C-NTO, A-NTO, and H-NTO were 2.89, 2.98, and 3.05&nbsp;eV, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b</a>, Supporting Information). The XPS valence band spectrum results in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16c,d</a> (Supporting Information) indicate that the <i>E</i><sub>g</sub> of H-NTO (2.79&nbsp;eV) is significantly lower than those of A-NTO (2.97&nbsp;eV) and C-NTO (3.27&nbsp;eV, see Supporting Information for the detailed calculation process). The narrowing of the forbidden band is attributed to oxygen vacancies and the doping effect of the heteroelements, which lead to a lower energy barrier for electrons to jump from the valence band to the conduction band, resulting in a significant shift of the absorption edge to lower energies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0002_R_d151980020e704" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0025_R_d151980020e704" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Appl. Phys. Lett.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These changes were also reflected in the colors of the samples, where NTO was initially white but turned light gray and gray after annealing in Ar and Ar/H<sub>2</sub>, respectively (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a>, Supporting Information). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface areas and corresponding pore size distributions of all the materials were estimated from N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption tests (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). H-NTO had the smallest specific surface area and C-NTO had the largest specific surface area, probably because the material annealed in the air had the least nitrogen doping, resulting in the smallest mass; therefore, the C-NTO material also had the most extensive pore size distribution.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202301376-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202301376-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Energy Storage Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>A 2032 half-cell was assembled using metallic Na as the counter electrode and the prepared samples as working electrodes to test the electrochemical performance. The Na-storage mechanism of the prepared samples was investigated by cyclic voltammetry (CV). For the CV curves of H-NTO shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0003">3a</a>, the irreversible reduction peaks at ≈0.97&nbsp;V during the initial cycle disappear in subsequent cycles, which are attributed to the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0002_R_d151980020e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater.">2</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0007_R_d151980020e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cathode peak near 1.43&nbsp;V and the anode peak at 1.21&nbsp;V in the first cycle belong to the pseudocapacitance Na<sup>+</sup> storage process, and they gradually disappear in the subsequent cycles, which can be attributed to the surface passivation film effect. The cathodic peak at 0.43&nbsp;V was due to the intercalation reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> and the reduction of Ti<sup>4+/3+</sup>. The peak shifted to 0.57 and 0.47&nbsp;V during the second and fifth cycles, respectively, probably due to the potential deflection produced by the electrode polarization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0026_R_d151980020e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Electrochem. Commun.">26</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0027_R_d151980020e747" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The oxidation peak at 0.51&nbsp;V corresponds to the extraction reaction of Na<sup>+</sup> and the oxidation of Ti<sup>3+/4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0027_R_d151980020e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These pair of redox peaks approached each other as the cycles proceeded, leading to a lower polarization voltage, a phenomenon that can be attributed to the kinetic activation and stabilization process of the SEI layer. From the second scan onward, all the curves overlapped very well, indicating excellent electrochemical reversibility. All the CV curves exhibited almost the same shape, suggesting the typical sodium storage characteristics of NTO, and compared to the CV curves of the A-NTO and C-NTO samples (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information), the CV curves of H-NTO had a significantly better coincidence. These results suggest that the H-NTO electrode has better electrochemical reversibility, which may be attributed to the higher oxygen vacancy concentration on the surface of the material, which provides more reversible Na-ion adsorption sites. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0003">3b</a>, the H-NTO electrode could be stably cycled for 3000 cycles (330 calendar days) at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention rate of 77.6% and an average capacity loss rate of only 0.0075% per cycle, which is an imposing value. The first charge/discharge specific capacity of the H-NTO electrode at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is 153/312&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the initial Coulomb efficiency (ICE) of 49% (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information). The initial charge/discharge specific capacities of A-NTO and C-NTO electrodes are 119/271 and 173/443&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and the corresponding ICE is only 44% and 39%, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b,c</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the A-NTO and C-NTO electrodes could only cycle 2000 cycles at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the specific capacity and capacity retention rate were much lower than those of the H-NTO electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19d,e</a>, Supporting Information). We also tested the cycle performance of the H-NTO electrode at 0.5 and 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and found that it can reach a capacity retention rate of more than 70% after 8&nbsp;000 cycles, and the specific capacity maintains high values (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c</a> (Supporting Information), the H-NTO electrode delivered a specific capacity of 19&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;°C, which was much higher than those of the A-NTO and C-NTO electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a,b</a>, Supporting Information). With the increase in temperature, the rate performance of the H-NTO electrode also gradually improved, and when the temperature reached 60&nbsp;°C, H-NTO could deliver 59&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and capacity retention of 96.6% (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). This result also demonstrates the excellent high-temperature electrochemical performance of the H-NTO electrode, which is attributed to accelerated electron/ion transfer at high temperatures. This is also demonstrated by the cycle performance at high temperatures, where the H-NTO electrode can maintain a long-cycle life and high capacity retention at both 40 and 60&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, &nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). The long-term cycling performance of the H-NTO electrode was tested at room temperature. Surprisingly, the H-NTO electrode can continuously and stably cycle for 80000 cycles without capacity degradation at a high current density of 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is a remarkable performance and a record in terms of cycle life. As a comparison, the long-cycle performance of A-NTO and C-NTO is far inferior to that of H-NTO (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information). We also summarized some literature reports on the electrochemical performance of Ti-based materials, and the cycling stability of the H-NTO electrode was far superior to them (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21d</a>, Supporting Information). This excellent long-cycle stability is attributed to the stable frame structure, unique chemical bonding interface, and electrolyte/electrode interface properties.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202301376-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f47870a3-9c51-49bf-90da-dd4b031fdfe7/adma202301376-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f47870a3-9c51-49bf-90da-dd4b031fdfe7/adma202301376-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fb70eacb-84ec-4b61-8682-7b650af5e404/adma202301376-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f47870a3-9c51-49bf-90da-dd4b031fdfe7/adma202301376-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202301376-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202301376" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical characterizations of the H-NTO electrodes. a) The CV curves for the first three cycles at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, b) the cycle performance at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, c) the rate performance at the different temperatures, d) the long cycle performance at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, e the cycle performance at −40&nbsp;°C of H-NTO electrodes, f) full cells schematic, g) the charge/discharge curves, and h) the cycle performance of full cells at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The low-temperature electrochemical performance of the H-NTO was tested using electrolyte engineering. Five electrolytes were selected, among which 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> in DIGLYME (1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaOTF in G2) showed the best electrochemical performance (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information), so 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaOTF in G2 was used as the electrolyte for all low-temperature electrochemical performance tests (a more detailed discussion of the low-temperature electrolytes is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5</a>). At higher current densities, the H-NTO electrode still cycles stably for 10000 cycles and delivers a specific capacity of 51&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). Even at −40&nbsp;°C, the H-NTO electrode maintains extraordinary electrochemical stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0003">3e</a>, 3000 cycles at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to 200 calendar days) and high rate performance (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). Such excellent electrochemical performance prompted us to further assemble a full cell to test the H-NTO electrode for practical applications (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0003">3f</a>). The full cell delivers 600 and 6500 cycles at 0.1 and 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, in the voltage range of 1.5–3.8&nbsp;V at 25&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0003">3g,h</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information), while also delivering a maximum power density of 4.5&nbsp;KW&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> and a maximum energy density of 180&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;Kg<sup>−1</sup> based on the empirical model (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0028_R_d151980020e913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Eng. J.">28</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0029_R_d151980020e913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Energy Lett.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This excellent rate performance led us to investigate the kinetics of the electrode material (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31–34</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to the A-NTO and C-NTO electrodes, the H-NTO electrode exhibited higher pseudocapacitive behavior, lower charge transfer impedance, faster ion-diffusion rate, and lower activation energy, indicating that a more significant oxygen vacancy concentration favors electron transfer and ion transport.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202301376-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202301376-sec-0050-title">2.3 Ex Situ Physicochemical Characterizations</h3>
            
            <p>To gain a deeper understanding of the Na-storage mechanism and its details, we performed ex situ XRD, XPS, and Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) tests on the H-NTO electrodes under different states of charge (SOC). The electrodes under different SOCs marked with colored dots in the charge/discharge curves were collected for ex situ measurements. All the XRD patterns exhibited similar shapes for different SOCs, with little change in the positions of all the diffraction peaks and no new diffraction peaks being generated, implying that the H-NTO electrode underwent no phase change during the charging/discharging process (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0004">4a</a>). Because of the excellent cycling stability of the H-NTO electrode, we evaluated the volume change of H-NTO at different SOCs. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35a</a> (Supporting Information), the layer spacings of the (001) and (020) crystalline surfaces increased and decreased, respectively, due to Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and release. Crystal volume was also evaluated. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35b</a> (Supporting Information) shows that H-NTO expands by only 3.9% in volume after complete Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and returns to its initial volume after complete Na<sup>+</sup> release. These values suggest that the H-NTO electrode has a low volume expansion effect during the charge/discharge process. This conclusion was confirmed by ex situ SEM observations and EIS tests of the electrodes with different numbers of cycles. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a> (Supporting Information), the surface of the H-NTO electrode with different numbers of cycles did not produce apparent cracks, the morphology remained the same as the initial morphology, and R<sub>ct</sub> did not increase significantly from 100 to 20000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37a</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, we detected only a small amount of Ti<sup>0</sup> in the electrode species after 20000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37b,c</a>, Supporting Information), which may explain the excellent cycling stability of the H-NTO electrode. Through an ex situ XPS test, we also characterized the valence change of Ti during charging and discharging. The Ti<sup>3+</sup> content increases significantly when discharging to 0.1&nbsp;V and then decreases significantly when charging to 2.5&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0004">4b</a>). This is consistent with our previous explanation that the charging and discharging processes involve only the release of Na<sup>+</sup> from H-NTO and the redox reaction between Ti<sup>3+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202301376-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aa742704-f05d-46bf-98b2-151d24a8b2f9/adma202301376-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/aa742704-f05d-46bf-98b2-151d24a8b2f9/adma202301376-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ac7965eb-9b3b-4126-9d08-80da57450bc8/adma202301376-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aa742704-f05d-46bf-98b2-151d24a8b2f9/adma202301376-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202301376-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202301376" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization of Na-storage mechanisms at room/low temperatures. a) Electrochemical ex situ XRD patterns and b) ex situ XPS patterns at 25&nbsp;°C for H-NTO//Na half-cell. c) Electrochemical ex situ XRD patterns and d) ex situ FTIR spectra for H-NTO//Na half-cell. e) Illustration of Na<sup>+</sup>–solvent co-intercalation behavior in H-NTO at −40&nbsp;°C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We also explored the Na-storage mechanism at low temperatures owing to the excellent cycle stability of the H-NTO electrodes. The literature has reported that the desolvation process is a crucial determinant of the rate performance due to the high desolvation energy (<i>E</i><sub>des</sub>) barrier at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0030_R_d151980020e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">30</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0031_R_d151980020e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Graphite anodes exhibit ion-solvent co-intercalation behavior at low temperatures, <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0032_R_d151980020e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and we speculate that NTO with a large interlayer spacing may have similar ion-solvent co-intercalation behavior to avoid the desolvation process and thus achieve fast kinetics at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0033_R_d151980020e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0034_R_d151980020e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Energy Lett.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0004">4c</a>, the diffraction peak at 10° underwent a significant position change during the charging and discharging processes, which indicated that the Na<sup>+</sup> was inserted and released, leading to a significant lattice distortion of H-NTO. Considering the excellent rate performance of the H-NTO electrodes at low temperatures, we suspect that ion-solvent co-intercalation may have caused extensive expansion and contraction of the NTO crystal structure. Therefore, we performed ex situ FTIR tests and observed significant peaks for the G2 solvent at 858&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> compared to the initial electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0004">4d</a>). If the solvent is only adsorbed on the material surface, the signal is independent of the charge state and can be detected during the entire charge/discharge process. Therefore, these test results indicate that charging and discharging at low temperatures are accompanied by an ion-solvent co-intercalation effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0035_R_d151980020e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nanomicro Lett.">35</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0036_R_d151980020e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Eng. J.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This prevents the desolvation of solventized Na ions and dramatically increases the Na-storage capacity of H-NTO at low temperatures. The H-NTO electrodes maintained excellent cycling stability even when subjected to significant volume expansion and contraction at low temperatures, largely owing to their stable framework architecture and accelerated ion transport by doping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0004">4e</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202301376-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202301376-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrode/Electrolyte Interface</h3>
            
            <p>An Ar<sup>+</sup>-etched XPS analysis of the electrode after cycling at room temperature was performed to reveal the composition of the SEI layer formed in the electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a>, Supporting Information). The C1s spectrum was fitted to five peaks. The peaks at 284.4, 285, 285.9, 287.4, and 289.5&nbsp;eV were attributed to C<span class="icomoon"></span>C, C<span class="icomoon"></span>O, C≐O, O≐C<span class="icomoon"></span>OH, and <span class="icomoon"></span>CF<sub>2</sub><span class="icomoon"></span>, respectively, and these organic components in the SEI were mainly derived from the decomposition of the solvent (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5a</a>) .<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0037_R_d151980020e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano Energy">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5b</a>, the F signal was detected both at the electrode surface and inside, and the peak at 684.5&nbsp;eV was attributed to Na-F, indicating the presence of NaF. Furthermore, the distribution of F was confirmed using STEM-HAADF mapping (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5c</a> shows that the contents of C and F decrease and increase with etching depth, respectively, indicating the alternating presence of organic–inorganic boundary layers. This integrated organic–inorganic boundary layer is the most stable layer in rechargeable batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0038_R_d151980020e1075" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5d</a>, the TEM test results revealed the formation of an SEI layer with an average thickness of 30&nbsp;nm. The presence of an organic-inorganic layer was also observed in the SEI layer, which is consistent with the XPS results. The NaF-rich SEI layer effectively inhibited the oxidation of the electrolyte and the corrosion/solvent of the electrode, supporting the long-cycle stability of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0039_R_d151980020e1083" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The organic component in the SEI has been reported to be more elastic and buffer mechanical deformation, thus providing good protection for the H-NTO electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0040_R_d151980020e1088" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Joule">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is clear that a homogeneous organic component is more conducive to interfacial stability, which is why the H-NTO electrodes exhibited excellent cycle stability in ester-based electrolytes.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202301376-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/200689c0-af69-4e01-9465-d5f6128c213a/adma202301376-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/200689c0-af69-4e01-9465-d5f6128c213a/adma202301376-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e0f70fb5-bb6a-42f6-bf6a-24847a22a13a/adma202301376-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/200689c0-af69-4e01-9465-d5f6128c213a/adma202301376-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202301376-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202301376" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Characterization theoretical calculation of SEI films and electrolytes. Component analysis of SEI formed in NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DEC electrolyte. a,b) XPS depth-profiles spectra for C 1s (a) and F 1s (b). c) Atomic ratio of C, F, and Na in SEI formed for cycled H-NTO electrode. d) TEM images of H-NTO electrode to detect SEI thickness (inorganic species are marked in the figure). e) HOMO−LUMO simulation figures of different salts and solvents, f) HOMO−LUMO and HOMO′−LUMO′ energy (Δ<i>E</i> values) of (PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>) Na<sup>+</sup>-2(G1, G2, G4) before (Na<sup>+</sup>) or after (Na<sup>+</sup>) one extra electron obtained from the electrode. Insets are LUMO and HOMO′ simulation figures. g) Comparative Raman spectrum, schematic interaction of ions (Na<sup>+</sup>, CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>) and solvent. h) Raman spectra of G2 at different temperatures. i) Desolvation energy of Na<sup>+</sup> in different solvents.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>It is well known that the ion transport rate will be significantly slowed down at low temperatures, which seriously affects the low-temperature electrochemical performance of the battery. The high freezing point of ester electrolytes and the formation of a thick SEI layer further slow ion transport. Therefore, the low-temperature electrochemical performance of the electrodes is highly dependent on the choice of the electrolyte. Additives in the electrolyte, <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0041_R_d151980020e1146" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Sci.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the introduction of highly conductive materials as cladding layers, <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0042_R_d151980020e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and doping with heterogeneous elements<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0043_R_d151980020e1156" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> can mitigate electrochemical performance degradation at low temperatures; however, capacity degradation is still inevitable. A research group led by Goodenough has achieved the long-cycle performance of SIBs at −25&nbsp;°C by using organic electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0044_R_d151980020e1162" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although this research is essential, cycle-performance degradation is inevitable in batteries that operate for long periods in cold climates. Therefore, electrolyte modification alone is insufficient to address the slow kinetic behavior of the electrodes at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0045_R_d151980020e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nat. Commun.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the excellent electrochemical performance of the H-NTO electrode at low temperatures is achieved by the precise construction of the electrode materials, and the precise adaptation of the electrolyte is of great significance. This encouraged us to continue exploring the most effective low-temperature electrolyte. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5e</a>, ether solvents have higher lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) levels than ester solvents, indicating that ether solvents are more difficult to reduce and favor the generation of thin and dense SEI dominated by inorganic substances. Although the LUMO energies of Na<sup>+</sup> and the solvent decreased after the formation of the solventized sheath in the electrolyte, the LUMO level of the Na<sup>+</sup>[ether][CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>] sheath was also higher than that of the other complexes (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S40</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, we found that the <i>E</i><sub>(HOMO’–LUMO)</sub> of Na<sup>+</sup>–G2–CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> was 1.385&nbsp;eV, which was significantly higher than that of the other solvent–salt complexes, indicating the higher stability of NaOTF/G2 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5f</a>). The low dielectric constant of the ether electrolyte also brought the CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> anion closer to the Na<sup>+</sup>–G2 unit (<i>ε</i><sub>EC</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;89.78, <i>ε</i><sub>G2</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6.3) .<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0046_R_d151980020e1219" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the Na<sup>+</sup> solvent unit accepts an electron, the anion can reduce the electronegativity change in the solvent, leading to an increase in the stability of the Na<sup>+</sup> solvent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0047_R_d151980020e1229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Energy Lett.">47</a>, <a href="#adma202301376-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0048_R_d151980020e1229" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Energy Lett.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectroscopy results also support this conclusion. The Raman peak of the G2-based electrolyte was most significantly red-shifted compared to that of the EC/DEC-based electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5g</a>). In contrast, fully solventized CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> can be kept far away from Na<sup>+</sup> in EC/DEC-based electrolytes because of its higher dielectric constant. After the dissolution of NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> salt in G2, the G2-solvated CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> can be kept very close to Na<sup>+</sup> due to the lower dielectric constant of G2. However, the Coulombic interaction between solvated CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> is weak because of the barrier of the G2 solvent, which makes it difficult for CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> to form Na<sup>+</sup>–CF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> ion pairs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0048_R_d151980020e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Energy Lett.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The low-temperature performance of the configured cryogenic electrolyte was tested using in situ Raman spectroscopy. The NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/G2 maintained a stable structure from −60 to 25&nbsp;°C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5h</a>). To investigate the co-insertion of the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent in H-NTO, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0005">5i</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S41</a> (Supporting Information), we calculated the <i>E</i><sub>des</sub> of the solvent molecules in the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complexes. The results showed that Na<sup>+</sup> has the highest <i>E</i><sub>des</sub> in ether solvents, and such a considerable <i>E</i><sub>des</sub> promotes the co-insertion of the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent in H-NTO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0049_R_d151980020e1317" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The synergistic effect of low-temperature electrolytes and electrode materials allows H-NTO to maintain stable cycle performance at low temperatures.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202301376-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202301376-sec-0070-title">2.5 Theoretical Calculations and Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to better understand the relationships between oxygen defects, heterogeneous elemental doping, and Na storage. Based on the experimental results, we constructed four models, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S42</a> (Supporting Information): NTO, NTO with oxygen vacancies (O<sub>v</sub>-NTO), C- and N-doped NTO (CN-NTO), and NTO with oxygen vacancies and C and N doping (CNO<sub>v</sub>-NTO). We first confirmed the formation sites of the oxygen vacancies, and the lowest oxygen vacancy formation energy was found at site 1 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S43</a>, Supporting Information). The oxygen vacancies of the subsequent models were all located at Site 1, and the cell parameters of the models did not change significantly after C and N doping, implying that the materials had good stability. Ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) calculations of the CNO<sub>v</sub>-NTO structure at 1000&nbsp;K for 10&nbsp;ps showed that the model was very stable in terms of energy and structure in the presence of defects and doping (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0006">6a</a>). The length of the Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0006">6b</a>, was also significantly reduced after doping; thus, the CNO<sub>v</sub>-NTO structure was more stable. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0006">6c</a> shows a crystal orbital Hamiltonian layout (COHP) analysis of the Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond interactions in the Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O medium based on these three structures.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202301376-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b89c80eb-9a35-4a6e-8feb-656ea3ed7e33/adma202301376-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b89c80eb-9a35-4a6e-8feb-656ea3ed7e33/adma202301376-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4a96fcda-91ef-4e10-a1af-f815b801baff/adma202301376-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b89c80eb-9a35-4a6e-8feb-656ea3ed7e33/adma202301376-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202301376-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202301376" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical calculation and analyses for the NTO-base electrode. a) Trend graph of temperature and system energy of H-NTO calculated using AIMD. b) Optimized structures of NTO, CN-NTO, O<sub>v</sub>-NTO, and CNO<sub>v</sub>-NTO and calculated bond lengths of Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds. c) Average pCOHP and the corresponding integral patterns for O<sub>v</sub>-NTO and CNOv-NTO (pCOHP is the projected crystal orbital Hamilton population and ICOHP is the integrated COHP). d) Adsorption energy (Δ<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) for Na storage, and e) total DOS of NTO with defects. f) Schematic illustration for the diffusion path of Na atom in the NTO, O<sub>v</sub>-NTO, and CNO<sub>v</sub>-NTO, and g) corresponding diffusion energy barrier.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>We found that the integral COHP (ICOHP) of CNOv-NTO near the Fermi energy level was more significant than that of other materials, implying that introducing defects and doping will help strengthen the Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds and stabilize the structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202301376-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202301376-bib-0001_R_d151980020e1396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Energy band and density of states (DOS) calculations also showed that introducing defects and doping improved the electrical conductivity of the material. A significant bandgap exists in the bare CNO<sub>v</sub>-NTO model near the Fermi energy level but disappears in the rest of the model system (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0006">6d</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S44</a>, &nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S45</a>, Supporting Information). This result suggests that the DOS near the Fermi energy level is enhanced owing to defects and doping, which increases the electronic conductivity and number of unpaired electrons near the Fermi energy level, leading to an increase in the reactivity of the material and enhanced adsorption of Na. As a result, both ion and electron transport were greatly accelerated. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0006">6e</a>, the CNO<sub>v</sub>-NTO model exhibits larger adsorption energy values than the other models, indicating that abundant defects and doping enable stronger adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> at the electrode. Subsequently, we investigated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion behavior in the intermediate layers of the three models under different diffusion states. It can be noted that the CNO<sub>v</sub>-NTO model presents a minimum value of 0.1&nbsp;eV at the position of the highest diffusion energy barrier, which is significantly lower than the corresponding 0.5 and 0.8&nbsp;eV for NTO and CN-NTO, indicating that the diffusion of Na ions between the CNOv-NTO layers is much easier (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202301376-fig-0006">6f,g</a>). These results suggest that substitutional doping and defects in the NTO system significantly increased the electronic conductivity, reduced the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier, and enhanced the structural stability, thus improving the electrochemical properties of NTO.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202301376-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202301376-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We have developed H-NTO microspheres covered by ultrathin 2D nanosheets containing abundant oxygen vacancies and unique chemically bonded interfaces. The robust structure ensured the stability of the electrode during long cycling, and changes in the physicochemical properties caused by the abundant oxygen vacancies and heterogeneous element doping improved the electrochemical performance of the H-NTO electrode. Optimizing the electrolyte achieved excellent cycling stability of the H-NTO electrode at −40°C. This study emphasizes the importance of interface, defect, and electrolyte engineering in the construction of highly durable Ti-based anode materials and wide-operation-temperature batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
